Chapter 10: Why I Am So Clever, Why I Write Such Excellent Books, and What I Did to the Gods: HemÄdri Reimagines BrÄhmaá¹‡ical Thought

In a manner that has few parallels in the long history of Sanskrit thought, especially in the often-subdued domain of DharmaÅ›Ästra, to read the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i is to stage an encounter with the mind of its author in all his richness of inspiration and many idiosyncrasies. Even after a decade of diligent research seeking out precedents across disciplines and religious boundaries, I have been forced to conclude that while HemÄdri is indeed a polymath with access to immense libraries, who may well have known a myriad of intellectual resources now lost to history, both at the level of doctrine and methodology, the most essential features of his work seem to be expressions of his own individual vision and outsized personality. The scope of this accomplishmentâ€”that reading HemÄdri is to hear one manâ€™s voiceâ€”is all the more extraordinary when we take into account that the vast majority of his text is a collection of citations drawn from the writings of other people, sages, and deities, and that even among its enthusiasts, dharmanibandha is as a genre is almost universally characterized as one of the most colorless discourses in all of Sanskrit.

If in chapter 9 we had focused on HemÄdriâ€™s interventions into preexisting social worlds, from here on in, our attention will be largely redirected to the inner worlds that lie inside his texts. While at first glance this might seem to represent a stark departure from the markedly sociological methodological focus and theoretical orientation that has animated the rest of our study, as if they are being abruptly discarded in the service of a more rarefied form of â€œpureâ€ intellectual history, such appearances are deceiving. For, like the scholastic VedÄntins from whom he derives so much theological inspiration, HemÄdri asserts that we must collapse the distinctions between how we read and how we live such that one becomes [[P1565]] an extension of the other. In a world where all daily activity is unavoidably punctuated by ritual, what the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i offers, in essence, is a hermeneutics for everyday life.

Though its tactics are indebted to PÅ«rva MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ, quite unlike with the dharma norms of classical legal traditions, the theology and presuppositions of the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i are those of transactional, quotidian, common sense, everyday Indian religions. For the first time in the history of â€œSmÄrtaâ€ traditions, this ethos is articulated and defended against its scholastic objectors in the language of high Å›Ästra. In a sense, at the very moment that he is taking away the authority of ordinary people to internally manage their own religious affairs without resorting to an intermediary from outside the community, HemÄdri goes to enormous trouble to affirm what was already obvious to ordinary peopleâ€”namely, that they lived in a world that was really full of gods and spirits who had to be fed, attended, and appeased, and that ritual was the means for making that happen. As counterintuitive as it might seem, just in doing this much, HemÄdri has already turned his back on the tradition of legal exegesis. As we shall soon see, DharmaÅ›Ästra as a scholastic tradition by and large had at least partially internalized MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄâ€™s realist and atheistic values alongside its methods. Ultimately, what we will discover is that to pay close attention to how HemÄdri reads and how he makes use of his sources throughout the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i in and of itself cannot but help to bring vividly into our awareness, in both broad strokes and particulars, how comprehensively HemÄdri breaks away from his predecessors within the domains of DharmaÅ›Ästra and formal theology.

In the final portions of the chapter, through examining and even regionalizing the key antecedents of the dharmanibandha discourse that HemÄdri so capaciously makes his own, the insights we have recovered from our close readings will be placed into a wider socio-historical context. We will see that such a broad-based historical perspective once again speaks to the [[P1566]] relative marginality within the wider purview of eleventh- and twelfth-century north Indian DharmaÅ›Ästra of much that to our author holds most sacred. In particular, the chapter will conclude by rendering transparent that the north Indian nibandhakÄra Laká¹£mÄ«dhara shares with his successor neither his valorization of personalist VedÄnta as the crowning achievement of Indic thought nor his investment in devotional theology. These observations in turn will prepare us for the final chapter in our study, which will take as its focus precisely that peculiar marriage of non-dual VedÄntic philosophical reasoning with affective devotionalism, the articulation of which, though already evident in the CaturvargacintamÄá¹‡i, comes to play a far more consequential role in the final stage of HemÄdriâ€™s intellectual life.

That HemÄdri does not follow the well-trod paths of his predecessors is a reflection of the centrality of theism to his worldview in general and his theory of scripture in particular. As this chapter will make evident, it is a vision that centers sacred personalities, their actions, and their assumptions as inseparable from what makes ritual efficacious. And so, in this spirit, before we can think with our texts, we need to take some time and get know HemÄdri SÅ«ri himself and the world from which he emerged.

Why I am So Clever: Autobiography of the Pandit as an Egomaniac

HemÄdri commences1 the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i by celebrating as a collective total â€œthe BrÄhmaá¹‡as who are praised within the three worldsâ€ (trijagatÄ«vandyÄ jayanti dvijÄá¸¥). The best of BrÄhmaá¹‡as, we learn,

While that is all well and good, however, special recognition is to be reserved for the crest jewel of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a sages, devoid of the vices of attachment, enmity, and intoxication, the muni known of as Vikhas (vikhÄbhidhÄno muniá¸¥). This is the source, our author will soon inform us, of his family gotra (gotre tasya).2

In speaking of Vikhas, HemÄdri presumably intends to invoke the sage who revealed the scriptures of the VaikhÄnasas. As Laurie Patton has discussed, the comparatively late but recognizably Vedic Taá¹‡á¸ya BrÄhmaá¹‡a contains a short narrative in which the Vedic á¹›á¹£is are said to emerge from the spilt seminal fluid that PrajÄpati offers into the sacrificial fire. Even so, at least in light of the sources available to us, HemÄdriâ€™s invocation of this pedigree is immediately suspect. In no other source available at present is the á¹›á¹£i from whom the line descends assigned the name Vikhas. Indeed, analogous to how a term like BhÄrgava means the â€œones who descend from Bhá¹›gu,â€ the otherwise barely attested word â€œVikhasâ€ seems to have been derived artificially by treating VaikhÄnasa as a [[P1568]] patronymic and undoing the application of vá¹›ddhi rules. The problem here is that both the Vedic BrÄhmaá¹‡as and the VaikhÄnasa scriptures themselves consistently tells us this sage is himself already named â€œVaikhanasa.â€ Furthermore, in the classical Sanskrit sources, VaikhÄnasa is classified as the name of a Vedic Å›ÄkhÄ. It is not a Vedic gotra, which is why VaikhÄnasa practitioners even today can belong to distinctive lines of Vedic textual transmission. In other words, for the first but hardly the last time in our sources, HemÄdriâ€™s explanation here reads like the efforts of a clever outsider looking in at a tradition and attempting to reverse engineer its contents based on more general Å›Ästric principles.

The CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i contains two garlands of verses where HemÄdri discusses and praises his family. Even among these two accounts contained within the same treatise, there are some interesting discrepancies. While the Vratakhaá¹‡á¸a begins with an account of HemÄdriâ€™s grandfather, VÄsudeva, descending from the line of Vikhas, the ÅšrÄddhakalpa starts by introducing us to the man who was presumably HemÄdriâ€™s paternal great-grandfather, a gentleman named VÄmana, said to be born in the lineage of the dear sage (vatsamuni), as the text says explicitly, in the Vatsa Gotra.4 This is to say that VÄmana is assigned to an entirely different gotra than the one which had earlier been claimed on behalf of his son. Since gotra inheritance is patrilineal, this is utterly impossible. Again, the seams in this undivided and â€œeternalâ€ BrÄhmaá¹‡ism are showing.

Regardless of his origins, in the slightly clunky style to which we will soon grow accustomed, VÄmana is outlandishly praised. While in the Vratakhaá¹‡á¸a, unsurprisingly, HemÄdriâ€™s grandfather VÄsudeva is assigned a whole host of rather generic virtues, the ÅšrÄddhakalpa sets out to represent VÄsudeva, a connoisseur of the secret substance of the eternal Upaniá¹£ads (Å›aÅ›vadvedarahasyavasturasikaá¹ƒ) as simultaneously a Vedic ritualist without peer and a master of the arts.5 In yet another heavily Å›leá¹£a-infused verse full of uneven metaphors, VÄsudeva is exalted as a person in whose Vedic sacrifice/scholarly society (goá¹£á¹­hÄ«sadaá¹ƒ) there is a piling up/compilation of the sacrificial components (kalÄsambhÄra) of the nine fires/freshly radiant arts (navojjvala)â€”navojjvala-kalÄsambhÄra-goá¹£á¹­hÄ«sadaá¹ƒ. At every step, this unusual and highly specific rhetoric sets out to assign to a single person a confluence of what in earlier centuries were typically values and attributes assigned to discrete interpretive communities. We cannot of course wholly rule out that this eccentric cultural formation was indeed in evidence in the social realm where HemÄdri spent his youth. As we have seen throughout our study, especially prior to the thirteenth century, throughout the Deccan, site- and community-specific values were almost unimaginably diversified. Still, it is strange that unlike all of the other major figures in the almost incestuous orbit of the Seuá¹‡a court, including HemÄdriâ€™s chief disciple Bopadevaâ€”whose father studied in the line of BhÄskara, the grandfather in turn of ÅšÄraá¹…gadeva the Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡eÅ›vara who wrote the Saá¹…gÄ«taratnÄkaraâ€”we find no trace of any of HemÄdriâ€™s forebears in any of our sources, nor can we link them, however tangentially, with any preceptorial line. And in a rhetorical choice almost unheard of, except perhaps among the most transgressive lines of charismatic visionary yogins, HemÄdri himself never mentions a teacher. It is as if he and his values came out of nowhere. In contrast to the unabashed reverential attitudes HemÄdri displays towards the under specified deeds of his ancestors, there is considerable and surprising ambiguity in our polymathâ€™s representations of his own father, KÄmadeva. Once again, there is also a discrepancy between what we find at the opening of the CVC and how the same matters are addressed in the ÅšrÄddhakhaá¹‡á¸a. While in the Vratakhaá¹‡á¸a, KÄmadeva is somewhat generically lauded as a great philanthropist who was dharma incarnate, the ÅšrÄddhakalpa suggests a much stranger story:

From that one emerged the heap of insight/PrajÃ±ÄrÄÅ›i whose name was KÄmadeva, a connoisseur of good deeds, who even in times of calamity never abandoned true speech. [Even] his play at bantering was akin to fresh nectar. The entire world was pleased by his excellent speech, conduct, and morals.6

Keeping company with the best of noble teachers, having observed the mauÃ±jÄ«vrata, having married a wife by whom was enacted the rituals and household rites, that most noble person took on the fourth ÄÅ›rama in order to worship the jyotir that is Åšiva, who destroys the black magic that is the perceiveable and non-perceivable(?), the one who bestows comfort, just a little bit.7

HemÄdri acknowledges his father as a truthful and eloquent man, but his real emphasis, it seems, is on the fact that his sire bore two distinct names and engaged in very different activities at different phases in his life. While in his worldly life, the father was called KÄmadeva, it is strongly implied that after he took the fourth ÄÅ›rama and became a renunciate, KÄmadeva assumed the decidedly AtimÄrgic, maybe even quite specifically PÄÅ›upata, sounding initiatory name of PrajÃ±ÄrÄÅ›i. Almost as if he is damning with faint praise, in contradistinction to the outlandish feats accomplished by other members of his line, HemÄdri describes his father as following in the path of the best of teachers by taking the sacred thread, getting married, and then performing some ritual and social duties. Whereas as grandpa VÄmanaâ€™s deeds allegedly outshone Viá¹£á¹‡uâ€™s, despite some fine qualities, HemÄdri oddly presents his father KÄmadeva as fulfilling the bare minimum requirements for being BrÄhmaá¹‡a. With unmistakable condescensionâ€”perhaps even outright venomâ€”HemÄdri informs us that the newly minted PrajÃ±ÄrÄÅ›i dedicated himself to what seems to be a program of Åšaiva ritual [[P1572]] worship that had as its conceptual focus something akin to the practices ascribed to the siddha traditions we explored in the eighth chapter. As we have seen, these culminate in worshipping the deity in the form of the liá¹…ga of the void, the bindu, or a point of light. By worshipping Åšiva in the form of the light just a little bit (jyotiá¸¥ kiÃ±cid upÄsituá¹ƒ), his son signals resentfully, daddy wasted the end of his life. After this interlude, at long last, the text prepares us for meeting HemÄdri himself:

HemÄdri indeed was incarnated in his [KÄmadevaâ€™s] house in order to make the world free from poverty.

By whom can be described the marvelous deeds of HemÄdri, that most desired progeny who attained the state of being a wish-fulfilling tree? . . . .

He bears all the array of arts. He always gives hundreds of cows. Having the state of a king of the twice-borns, known across the world, he even bears the state of causing one to cross over.

Indeed, the remaining enemies of the Lord of the Earth, MahÄdeva, are cast down.

That supreme HemÄdri SÅ«ri, who has attained the state of being the overlord over the Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡as and also wisdom, is the moon that makes flourish the ocean of milk of the kingdom, set down both ÅšrÄ« and SarasvatÄ« in the houses and bodies of learned people.8

In violation of that Sanskrit ancient dictum, which proclaims that like the bickering co-wives whose names they bear, great fortune (Å›rÄ«) and great learning (sarasvatÄ«) cannot coexist in the same householdâ€”let alone the same personâ€”HemÄdri is emphatic that his coming into the world has made possible a new paradigm. From henceforth, eschewing the noble poverty of the gleaner as ideal, learned BrÄhmaá¹‡as will be guaranteed worldly success. What makes this new world possible, as we saw at some length in the prior chapter, is the confluence of a [[P1573]] new institutional form, the office of Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡eÅ›vara, which conflates the previously distinct roles of chief minister of the king (mahÄmÄtya) and administrator of the kingdom (Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡a). The end result is the marrying of the administrative, sacerdotal, and apotropaic functions of the kingâ€™s chief councilor and master of mantras with command over a bureaucratic apparatus possessing the type of intimate local knowledge necessary for ensuring the extraction of revenue, the management of debts, as well as battalions of scribes responsible for the reproduction of entextualized knowledge in all its forms. To be clear, this office has itself several centuries of prehistory highly localized to western Deccan. What it was waiting for was someone to harness its latent potential and bend it to his will. In HemÄdri SÅ«ri, the office of Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡eÅ›vara found just such a person.

When a single person holds so much power, with so very little opportunity for external critique or oversight, there is always the possibility of epistemic closure, that he will come to conflate his own limited perspective with the totality of possible human experiences. Typically, such observations preface a critique of the abuse of power. Here, however, they are offered in the spirit of hermeneutical charity, as providing a working framework for making sense of the otherwise inexplicable manner in which the most famous Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡eÅ›vara in the history of Indic thought represents himself to posterity. And so, just as the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i turns to its primary task, in introducing himself to his readers, HemÄdri writes:

Some people desire knowledge (jijÃ±ÄsÄ) about dharma. Others simply know, because theyâ€™ve read my books. And that just about sums up that â€œfieldâ€ in its entirety. And thus, by everyone without exception, there can be named no one else on the surface of the earth who ever was or will be more capable of knowing and acting than HemÄdri SÅ«ri.9

Virtually since its inception, much of Sanskrit Å›Ästra10 has perceived jijÃ±ÄsÄâ€”usually understood as â€œthe desire for [[P1574]] knowledgeâ€â€”as the impetus behind learned philosophical and theological inquiry. Whether the knowledge being sought after is a matter of understanding and annihilating the causes of suffering, as in SÄá¹ƒkhya KÄrikÄ 1.1 (duá¸¥khatrayÄbhighÄtÄj jijÃ±ÄsÄ tadabhighÄtake hetau), of making sense of the eligibility and impetus for performing ritual (athÄto dharmajijÃ±ÄsÄ) as in the MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ SÅ«tras, or of seeking out the method that enables one to realize brahman (athÄto brahmajijÃ±ÄsÄ), as in VedÄnta, the cultivation of this aptitude and attitude is seen as an essential dimension of what it means to be a learned and pious person. In fact, the question of just how precisely one should interpret jijÃ±ÄsÄ, whether what is at stake here is â€œdesireâ€ as a motivating factor, or, as Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya would have it, a dispassionate investigation, is utterly integral to how PÅ«rva and Uttara MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ present themselves as disciplines. And yet, with a wave of his hand, HemÄdri declares that his writings have rendered this entire dimension of human natureâ€”the quest for knowledgeâ€”antiquated and superfluous. Instead of yearning for something more, people should just read his books.

Now, Sanskrit discourse is not afraid to bluntly and incessantly tell its imagined reader, â€œlistenâ€”I am about to tell you a wonderous, unprecedented secret, something of fundamental importance that you have never heard before,â€ provided, that is, that it is the gods themselves who are imagined as our authors, or a work purports to entextualize a specific siddhaâ€™s channeled visionary experiences. In contrast, when it comes to the taxonomic and commentarial writings of humanâ€”all too humanâ€”scholars, it is customary for Sanskrit pandits to offer their intellectual and theological innovations surreptitiously while making a [[P1575]] very public show of diffidence to prior authorities within their discipline. Even when presenting an agenda that from the perspective of the modern historian of ideas diverges radically from what is found in the root text, Sanskrit pandits present their contributions coached in the dual languages of restorationâ€”let us return to what this author really was trying to sayâ€”as well as that of apologeticsâ€”here are five more arguments refuting attempts by rival schools of thought at discrediting our value system; in this way I have restored its honor. Poets, especially at the beginnings of their works, tend to be even more self-abnegating, such that KÄlidÄsa, one of the finest and most original writers in any language, after dubbing himself an â€œidiotâ€ (manda), compares his humble attempts at poetic composition to that of a dwarf (vÄmana) reaching out for a fruit hanging upon a branch that lies far out of his reach.11 When writers and poets undertake a rather bold project, by and large they claim its shortcomings as reflecting their own personal failings and ascribe any virtues residing in the end product to the grace and instruction of their teacher and to the gods.

These tropes and readerly expectations are hardly archaic. This is to say, HemÄdriâ€™s wholesale embrace of an idiom that is their antithesisâ€”exalting the author and his accomplishments and celebrating his innovations above all othersâ€”is not something that can be accounted for as a product of the times or as some quaint Maharashtrian custom. Thus, writing some forty years after the completion of the CaturvargacintamÄá¹‡i, fifteen miles from the BrÄhmaá¹‡ical center of Paithan and a mere thirty miles to the south of the YÄdava capital at Devgiri, JÃ±Ändev, perhaps the most gifted poet of his day, plaintively and apologetically still [[P1576]] hopes that if learned people encounter his Marathi GÄ«tÄ, they will respond to it the same way a mother applauds her child when it does what she has taught him,12 or a parrot when it recites something it has overheard.13 Time and again, acting almost like the antithesis of our author, JÃ±Ändev says he is but a marionette whose every movement reflects the guiding gestures of his guru and he pleads that the saints will help assist him in making sense of the Bhagavad GÄ«tÄ.

Nor can we make sense of HemÄdriâ€™s stylistic eccentricity through a discursive reflection on the specific genresâ€”medicine, poetics, devotional theology, DharmaÅ›Ästraâ€”in which he is working. Except in the case of his medical writings, though even there a rhetoric of newness predominates, as this chapter will demonstrate, the key formal and intellectual features of his compositions depart substantively from everything that has come before them. Against our usual expectations of the Sanskritic past that it is an emotionally distant realm governed by tropological systems that elide every hint of unvarnished human experience, HemÄdriâ€™s unforgettable voice as a writer speaks boldly and clearly down through the centuries. This voice is utterly inexplicable except when read as an organic expression of our authorâ€™s very particular and vivid personality.

It is perhaps in his least self-evidently innovative work, The Resurrection of Ä€yurveda (*Ä€yurvedaras## The Resurrection of Ä€yurveda

(Ä€yurvedarasÄyana), that the enormous disparity between HemÄdriâ€™s approach to engaging the scholastic traditions he has inherited and that of his immediate predecessors working within the very same genre is most starkly rendered.14 Completed somewhere between fifty and one [[P1577]] hundred years before HemÄdriâ€™s Ä€yurvedarasÄyana, the SarvÄá¹…gasundara of Aruá¹‡adatta also comments systematically on the eighth-century Aá¹£á¹­Äá¹…gahá¹›daya of VÄgbhaá¹­á¹­a. Here is how Aruá¹‡adatta introduces himself:

Aruá¹‡adattaâ€™s commentary then dives right into glossing the various parts of VÄgbhaá¹­á¹­aâ€™s opening maá¹…gala verse. This begins not with praise of the gods, but by honoring the doctor without precedent (apÅ«rvavaidyÄya namos tu tasmai).16 When he reaches its subject, the first or incomparable physician, Aruá¹‡adatta speaks of him as a man made of marvels (ÄÅ›caryabhÅ«ta)17 who is to be worshipped, for he takes away not only physical diseases such as fever, but the inner diseases of lust and delusion. As we are beginning to see, while representing the desire for inquiry (jijÃ±ÄsÄ) as a mere footnote to his own intellectual accomplishments was one of the more egregious examples of our authorâ€™s extraordinary hubris, it is entirely in keeping with the peculiar texture that define all of his works. Whereas Aruá¹‡adatta barely talks about himself at all, HemÄdri can barely stop talking about himself. Apart from a single opening verse in praise of Viá¹£á¹‡u, the very first word that appears in the â€œResurrection of Ä€yurvedaâ€ is â€œHemÄdri.â€ Against good sense and syntax, the opening verse in which this word appears not only somehow manages to invoke our illustrious authorâ€™s composition of the CaturvargacintamÄá¹‡i three times, but in doing so, it frames the entire purpose of the already ancient discipline of medicine as an adjunct to his [[P1579]] intellectual project in the CaturvargacintamÄá¹‡i. Next, we learn that while HemÄdri is willing to adhere to the doctrines taught by semi-mythologized long departed sages, figures like Caraka, HÄrÄ«ta, SuÅ›ruta, two of whose treatises quite literally define Ayurvedic practice, he is decidedly less diffident towards the commentators one might erroneously imagine to be his peers. Introducing for the first time the rather withering asides about the stupidity of all other members of the medical profession that are sprinkled throughout the body of his text, HemÄdri exclaims that esteemed figures like Haricandra and Jaijaá¹­a failed to arrive at the correct conclusions. The exceptionally difficult but compelling verse at the center of the passage we have just been examiningâ€”in which purging the human body of doá¹£ic imbalances is analogized with purging the body politic of unwanted social detritusâ€”does a masterful job of encapsulating some essential dimensions of HemÄdriâ€™s intellectual vision. Through this short passage from â€œThe Resurrection of the Ä€yurveda,â€ we have begun to become acclimated to our authorâ€™s much larger interpretive project, the theoretical and philosophical dimensions of which we will explore in greater detail in the next section. Much as the emergence of the office of the Å›rÄ«karaá¹‡eÅ›vara fuses together previously unrelated modes of authority at the macrocosmic level, HemÄdri reimagines the BrÄhmaá¹‡a ritualist as an all-purpose caretaker and administrator of the internally diverse ritual, medical, social, and moral [[P1580]] well-being of the wider society transcending the boundaries of religious identity and caste. As this chapter aims to demonstrate, though exceptionally familiar to usâ€”for this is what we tend to think of when we think of the place of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a in Indian society todayâ€”while the individual elements that make up this role have their own antecedents, the coherent articulation of this social identity within the mainstream of Å›Ästric discourse in general and DharmaÅ›Ästra in particular, is hardly in evidence before this particular moment in the history of Indic thought.

In fact, when the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡iâ€™s innovations are placed within the context of prior visions of Hindu law, the revolutionary nature of the textâ€™s intellectual and social program becomes emphatically clear. As will become evident in the middle of this chapter when we return to substantiating these claims, well into the twelfth century, BrÄhmaá¹‡ical DharmaÅ›Ästra had explicitly forbidden BrÄhmaá¹‡a ritualist-legalists from overseeing non-Vaidika ritual upon pain of outcasting. Succinctly, as we have seen before, the discoursesâ€™ responses to the theology and praxis of the Åšaiva age had taken two distinctive forms. In the vast majority of cases, following in the footsteps of the YÄjÃ±avalkyasmá¹›ti, DharmaÅ›Ästra was accommodationist, without diluting its own principles. As has been demonstrated, through implementing the principle of vyavasthÄ, the differential application of norms, and by recognizing the existence of plural modes of authority, it managed to carry out this project by carving out separately governedâ€”often quite vastâ€”social spaces where a vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma ethos was inapplicable. In the dissenting legal tradition, which we encountered a bit at the end of the last chapter, the Åšaiva Age was acknowledged by rejecting its legitimacy in toto. Primarily comprised of PÅ«rva MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ laden commentaries on the Manusmá¹›ti but also [[P1581]] including AparÄrkaâ€™s commentary on YÄjÃ±avalkya and the DÄnasÄgara of King BallÄlasena of Bengal, building upon KumÄrilaâ€™s arguments in the TantravÄrttika, this corpus simply denies any validity to the dominant religious paradigms of the early medieval world, thereby prescribing that BrÄhmaá¹‡a legalists have no constructive role to play within these religious ecologies. As we have seen, political power among the ruling classesâ€”to say nothing of their daá¹‡á¸anÄyaka enforcersâ€”was overwhelmingly in the hands of extended family networks that contained initiates into the MantramÄrga, especially in its more transgressive dimensions. As a practical consequence, despite the radically divergent value systems at work, both of these â€œBrÄhmaá¹‡icalâ€ approaches by and large yielded comparable counterintuitive outcomesâ€”namely, the exclusion of the Vaidika DharmaÅ›Ästrin from most of the subcontinentâ€™s religious life.

As we examined a bit in the prior chapter and will soon explore in even greater detail, building upon largely untheorized inclusivist tendencies in Laká¹£mÄ«dharaâ€™s Ká¹›tyakalpataru, which simply fold into the realm of Hindu law as supplements to its traditional canon the entire spectrum of PurÄá¹‡ic praxis as well as substantial elements of the ÅšÄkta and Åšaiva religious world, HemÄdriâ€™s CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i deliberately collapses the esoteric traditions of the early medieval world into the category of the PurÄá¹‡as. Then, through selective and emended citations, our text treats the resulting discourse as doctrinally univocal and perfectly in alignment with both traditional BrÄhmaá¹‡ical canons of knowledge as well as the ethos of the BhÄgavata PurÄá¹‡a. Rather than polemicizing against and thoroughly rejecting the systems of praxis that made up the Åšaiva Age, at the expense of the existing ecclesiastical establishment, HemÄdri assigns authority over the management of Åšaiva ritual lives, infrastructure building, and sacred geographies to BrÄhmaá¹‡a legalist ritualists who are not themselves initiates into the traditions of the MantramÄrga. Rendered redundant, the ruling religious powers of the day are then purged from the body politic. As was argued last chapter, to see this [[P1582]] development as a restoration of BrÄhmaá¹‡ical normativity after many centuries of diminished influenceâ€”a return to normalâ€”is a gross misreading of the historical record. For, as HemÄdri himself will tell us again and again, in both its method and conceptual framework, HemÄdriâ€™s reimagining of BrÄhmaá¹‡ical knowledge is self-consciously sui generis. That we do not instantly recognize the radical nature of this iconoclastic vision is merely a reflection of the stunning decree to which HemÄdri left his mark on the pages of the history of Indian religions.

HemÄdriâ€™s iconoclasmâ€”carried out in the service of shoring up the position of what will come to be the dehistoricized BrÄhmaá¹‡ical establishmentâ€”is beautifully illustrated by the closing portion of his final work, the KaivalyadÄ«pikÄ, or â€œThe Illuminator of Enstasy.â€ This treatise ostensibly offers a commentary on his pupil Bopadevaâ€™s MuktÄphalaâ€”â€œthe Garland of Pearlsâ€â€” which anthologizes, reframes, and interprets select verses extracted from the BhÄgavata PurÄá¹‡a. In its own right, the MuktÄphala fuses together one of the first concrete moments in the reception history of the theology of the BhÄgavata PurÄá¹‡a with the earliest systematic articulation of the discourse on bhakti rasa. While we will turn to a careful reading of the text in its own right, in our concluding chapter, for the moment what concerns us are not matters of devotional theology, poetics, and Advaita VedÄnta philosophy, but more circumscribed questions pertaining to form and style. Perhaps even more egregiously than the â€œgranthakartur praÅ›astiâ€ he had authored generations early, in almost every aspect, HemÄdriâ€™s chosen ending for both the MuktÄphala and â€œThe Illuminator of Enstasyâ€ flouts the inherited expectations of the proper way to organize a Å›Ästra and the conventions of socially acceptable â€œaucityaâ€ poetry. After wrapping up its discussion of bhakti in the mode of the heroic rasa, Bopadevaâ€™s text concludes with sixteen additional verses. Six of these take as their exclusive focus pun-laden praises of HemÄdri. Each and every one of them comes coupled with an extensive commentary in which HemÄdri offers profuse explications of his own seemingly inexhaustible greatness. The first verse runs as follows:

KaivalyadÄ«pikÄ: And here, as was stated previously, despite his own [HemÄdriâ€™s] authorship, the great minister of the king of the Yadus, HemÄdri, has made the petitioned ÄcÄrya [Bopadeva] declare authorship of it [the MuktÄphala]. Before we turn to HemÄdriâ€™s commentaryâ€”or should we say â€œauto commentaryâ€â€”on the verse itself, we need to pause and process what we have just learned. On the surface, it seemed that the MuktÄphala is a work by Bopadeva, HemÄdriâ€™s disciple, that opens and closes with extensive verses in praise of Bopadevaâ€™s teacher and his accomplishments.22 Though [[P1584]] the manner of praise is somewhat florid even by such standards, this arrangement would be perfectly in keeping with the normal approach to composing a Sanskrit text in classical, early medieval, and early modern South Asia. The existence of the KaivalyadÄ«pikÄ in and of itself at all is much less explicable. In South Asia, teachers do not typically compose commentaries on the work of their students. They certainly do not compose commentaries that in length and conceptual sophistication overshadow the root text. And they most definitely do not dedicate substantial portions of these commentaries to discussing themselves. As the sixth-century poetic theorist BhÄmaha, a stickler for traditional propriety, proclaims, â€œoneâ€™s own deeds (svacaritra) are to be related by others (anyaiá¸¥) and not by the protagonist (nÄyaka) himself, for how could a well-born person expound on his own qualities?â€23

By the time you reach the end of reading through a text by HemÄdri, you are likely to have become inured to such stylistic peculiarities. And so, you may not realize that making somebody else pretend they wrote a text that you yourself composed, just so you can write a commentary on the work that provides you with ample opportunity for self-praise, is no oneâ€™s idea of normal pretty much anywhere in the history of human discourse. Even more absurdly, in a rhetorical twist that seems like something out of Borges, both the verses and the autocommentary periodically break the fourth wall, for a moment informing the attentive [[P1585]] reader of the ruse that is being played on them, before abruptly returning to maintaining the extended fiction that the author of the root text and the commentary are completely different people. Keeping in mind our textâ€™s rather novel deployment of the author function, we are ready to turn to HemÄdriâ€™s exegesis of â€œBopadevaâ€™sâ€ verse offered in celebration of HemÄdri and his CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i.

That being the case, even up to the conclusion, he makes him [Bopadeva] relate his [HemÄdriâ€™s] fame, saying â€œby the clever. . .â€

â€œBy that mercantilism (vaá¹‡ijyayÄ) in the great ocean that is the text known as the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i.â€

Clever (caturaá¹‡a) means that he has learned a distinctive style of the composition of texts. Here is the example: when it comes to shipmanship, having gathered from various countries/locations in texts the accumulated precious objects/favored topics, a man becomes clever in those subjects. In exactly the same way, having assembled quotations that concern the entire teachings on vrata, dÄna, tÄ«rtha, moká¹£a, and so forth from the various Smá¹›tis, ItihÄsas, PurÄá¹‡as, etc., he [HemÄdri] has achieved skill in the composition of texts.

Then, by that very same delineation, having assembled various verses from the various skandhas of the BhÄgavata PurÄá¹‡a that are concerned with the teaching of the fourfold objects that are Viá¹£á¹‡u and so forth, he [HemÄdri] has made this prakaraá¹‡a text called the MuktÄphala. Clever means skillful.

[In this interpretation the meaning would be:] By HemÄdri, the wealthy merchant, these pearls that are the MuktÄphala are accumulated.24

It is worth our time for a moment to attend to the texture of the text. HemÄdriâ€™s verse is conceptually interesting. It makes use of an uncommon theme and attempts, as much of his poetry will do, to construct a Å›leá¹£a verse that deliberately yields two discrete interpretations. At the same time, there is a strong case to made that from the perspective of the conventions of classical Sanskrit poetics, this is, objectively speaking, very bad poetry. We can say this for two reasons. The first is basically formal. Properly formed, Å›leá¹£as are supposed to result in two equally grammatically and syntactically sound readings of a verse. Here in contrast, unless you treat the compounded phrase â€œcaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡ivaá¹‡ijyayÄâ€ as containing a completely elided but essential term, some notion of the oceanâ€”the initial interpretation of the verse makes no sense at all either semantically or syntactically. Indeed, this odd formation almost entirely serves the purpose of making possible the in many ways much more natural secondary reading of the text, where the proper title of HemÄdriâ€™s text is subdivided up into its discrete componentsâ€”such that we have HemÄdri, the clever merchant examining with care (cintÄ) gems (maá¹‡i) from the four classes (caturvarga), and he has dredged up (arjita) pearls (muktÄphala). In the hands of a better poet, the two interpretations of the verse would be more equally executed in a manner that better aligns with the demands of syntax. Even if we let this stylistic faux pas slide, whether we view the shipmanship of HemÄdri as enabling him to set sail upon either a magic gem or upon a voluminous work of DharmaÅ›Ästra, we are still left with a rather awkward mixed metaphor of the sort that Sanskrit literary critics abhor.

The other reason why this verse would be almost certainly have been condemned as a quintessential example of bad or inappropriate poetry (kukÄvya) within virtually any sabhÄ [[P1587]] dedicated to the formal study of Sanskrit poetics (Alaá¹ƒkÄraÅ›Ästra) is that it violates the cultural propriety of its day. Here it is important to remember that within courtly cultures, Sanskrit poetry is less a matter of â€œself-expressionâ€ than it is a vehicle for producing artisanal objects fit for critical evaluation. Just as was the case with philosophical debate, once it is completed, poetry is intended to be presented in public to be praised or picked to pieces by the learned assembly. While inspiration and the power to evoke strong emotions are certainly considered noteworthy, the appraising of poetry is largely a matter of properly identifying the qualities (guá¹‡a) and flaws (doá¹£a) of a particular work.

Some schools of thought (RÄjaÅ›ekhara for example is particularly generous) make allowances for captivating and sophisticated verses that occasionally transgress the inherited canon of literary norms. However, by and large, the discourse on poetics, particularly the increasingly dominant Kashmiri tradition, demands that in both its form and content verse must align with the conventions of the poets (kavisamaya). Just as sculptors and architects have their own in-community guild secrets, not readily shared with non-initiates, the conventions of the poets present themselves as a body of less than obvious in-house regulations governing how a literary work must be constructed. Much of this canon associates specific topics and imagery with particular emotional moods or correlates types of subjects and agents with the proper tropes and poetic imagery. For the Kashmiri tradition, which comes to dominate the history of poetics, both sets of concerns are seen as encapsulated in the discourse on literary propriety (aucitya). Now, when it comes to questions pertaining to the analysis of the bhÄvas, vibhavas, and sthayibhÄvas, especially as it pertains to dramaturgy, propriety (aucitya) is treated [[P1588]] comprehensively and explicitly in the surviving literature on poetic theory. Indeed, as we shall see in the next chapter, HemÄdriâ€™s writings cite liberally from across the spectrum of this discourse, drawing on the writings of Mammaá¹­a, Bhoja, Abhinavagupta, and, especially, DhanaÃ±jaya and his brother Dhanika. Indeed, the second half of the MuktÄphala and KaivalyadÄ«pikÄ are dedicated almost exclusively to offering an exhaustive exploration of the relevance of these canons for reconceptualizing the discourse on devotion in the technical language of Sanskrit poetics. In contrast, when it comes to questions of mastering the canon of poetic conventions in the service of understanding the appropriateness of deploying poetic techniques in specific contexts or assigning imagery to specific subjects, while the texts on poetics discuss the operative principles relatively clearly, they view the actual taxonomies in toto of appropriate and inappropriate associations as the sort of thing a poet needs to learn from his teacher. For this reason, the canon is not presented within the Å›Ästras. However, when a commentator particularly invested in understanding what makes poetry â€œbadâ€ turns to dismantling a sample verse at some length, invoking the maxims of practicing poets, as Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a will do in his exploration of the nature of the poetic flaws in the second chapter of the Vyaktiviveka, we can glean something of the rich and intricate nature of this largely unwritten discourse as well as the impossibility of an outsider to it simply inferring the myriad of rules that govern the game.

For those who believed that one of the main purposes of good poetry is to model the way that the world ought to beâ€”by teaching people to act like RÄma and not like RÄvaá¹‡a, and through assigning everything in the three worlds to its proper placeâ€”following these poetic conventions is no mere matter of displaying good taste, but an ethical imperative. Conversely, depicting in verse a non-idealized world full of inappropriate associations represents a massive moral failing. Because the rules governing literary practice have been imagined as comprised [[P1589]] of both entextualized and unentextualized standards, any outsider, however learned and brilliant, who inserted themselves into these discourses without undergoing the proper socialization would inevitably run afoul of breaking the hidden rules. They would therefore be deemed a bad poet, which is to say, a bad person. And just like, to use the canonical example, comparing the burly arm of BalarÄma with the soft petals of a lotus is â€œanaucitya,â€ because BalarÄma by his very nature must be depicted as hard and ripped and a lotus has neither of these traits, habitually homologizing a BrÄhmaá¹‡a with a merchant and the making of Å›Ästra with mercantile activities would have represented an appalling breach of etiquette from the perspective of the literati. Because these are domains that operate at different levels of excellence according to divergent principles, and kÄvya is supposed to present us with idealized representations of social forms and not the messy realities of the real world, equating BrÄhmaá¹‡a and merchant represents nothing less than a breach in the moral order. Itâ€™s the sort of thing that only an auteurâ€”and not a properly â€œeducatedâ€ member of the community in good standingâ€”would even dream of doing.

While in the absence of independent sources of documentary evidence, we can only engage in informed speculation, the irregularities in expression of the sort we have just been exploring found throughout all of HemÄdriâ€™s writings themselves offer a substantial body of circumstantial evidence suggesting, as we have suggested before, that our author champions a BrÄhmaá¹‡ical order into which he himself is only partially and unevenly acculturated. To put things another way, for all his literary aspirations, the new world that HemÄdri envisions is not a restoration of the logic that animated what Sheldon Pollock has called the Sanskrit cosmopolis but a replacement for it. In lieu of secret elitist conventions, HemÄdri advocates universalized standards. In place of cultivated etiquette, HemÄdri preaches values. And it is in [[P1590]] this spirit, having just told the reader â€œdrop what you are doing and read HemÄdriâ€™s books,â€ that the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i prepares us for its new dharma discourse.

Universalizing Hindu Dharma

Now, in order to remove the doubt (Å›aá¹…kÄ) of a world deprived of light, He, of exalted intellect, lays out the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i.25

That wish-fulfilling gem of unmeasurable virtue, which previously Mount Mandara churned forth from the ocean, generally grants much wealth/meaning (artha) from petitioning it. The chapters in this [work] are in order, the Vrata, DÄna, TÄ«rtha and Moká¹£a. What is fifth is the remainder (pariÅ›eá¹£a) division (khaá¹‡á¸a), where dharma shines undivided (akhaá¹‡á¸ita).

Before introducing us to the formal and conceptual structure of his text, HemÄdri pauses to homologize his dharmanibandha to the wish-fulfilling gem that came forth from the depths of the ocean of milk when it was churned by the gods and the demons. This narrative is alluded to so frequently in the text, almost always as part of an extended metaphor, that it is worth taking seriously what such a rhetorical choice implies. During the churning of the ocean (samudramanthana), the ocean gods and the dÄnavas, working together, entwined the NÄga [[P1591]] king VÄsuki, as if he were a rope, around the Mandara mountain. Plunging the mountain into the ocean of milk, they used this impromptu and unlikely contraption as a churning rod, with the aim of bringing forth the nectar of immortality for which the gods and demons alike yearned. In some variants of the storyâ€”though interestingly enough, not the BhÄgavataâ€™s tellingâ€”the nectar was proceeded by the wish-fulfilling cow, called KÄmadhenu or Surabhi, the wish-fulfilling tree, or Kalpataru, the wish-fulfilling gem (cintÄmaá¹‡i), and then, at long last, ÅšrÄ« bearing the pot of nectar. It is thus certainly no accident that this precise sequence matches in both name and order of appearance the earliest works of dharmanibandha known to HemÄdriâ€™s predecessor Laká¹£mÄ«dhara, whose own Ká¹›tyakalpataruâ€™s predecessors in turn were called the great ocean (MahÄrá¹‡ava) and KÄmadhenu. Quite unlike Laká¹£mÄ«dhara, however, who merely used this trope to carve out a place for himself in the discourse, in calling his text the CintÄmaá¹‡i, HemÄdri has positioned himself rhetorically as the culmination of an emergent tradition.

With the appearance of the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i, which fills in all the gaps in what came before, there is no need for good people to ever put forth exertion â€œelsewhere.â€ Like the BhÄgavata itself,26 our nibandha is both final â€œrevelation,â€ at least of a secular sort, as well as a comprehensive self-illuminating Å›Ästra. For above all else, by its own inherent power, what this text does is illumine the existing darkness that pervades the early medieval Deccan. With this new guiding light, which, against the pretenses of the Kaula niá¸¥Å›aá¹…kÄvÄda is what really removes doubt, we can differentiate the light from the dark, the right from the wrong, the pure from the impure. But more than that, with the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i as their guide, [[P1592]] â€œgood peopleâ€â€”in other words, the right sort of upright BrÄhmaá¹‡asâ€”can contribute to the greater project of restraining blind darkness, and those who embody it, at a distance. Furthermore, our author hints, the text also provides this audienceâ€”once again, the BrÄhmaá¹‡a DharmaÅ›Ästrinâ€”with access to modes of community-specific artisanal knowledge.

As we have seen in the previous chapter, with this vision in mind, the text now tells us how it is organized, namely, into four divisions. Of these, two survive mostly intact, a third is available in part, and the fourth, and for HemÄdri the most important, thus far seems lost forever. Finally, our author tells us, there is also an appendix, the PariÅ›eá¹£a division. Here, as he states enigmatically, â€œdharma shines undivided.â€ We have just seen that under the surface these seemingly simple verses all are subtly indicative of key facets of the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡iâ€™s overall interpretive project. Still, calling dharma â€œundividedâ€ is usually a mere truism, if not simply metrical filler. What precisely might HemÄdri be getting at he when invokes an akhaá¹‡á¸itadharma? To understand that, we have to first grasp his idiosyncratic and very specific redefinition of the dharma discourses.

HemÄdri writes:

Dharma is victorious, the sole cause of victory, of which they exemplify six types, due to its union with many intermediary particulars, among which there are also many others [subcategories.] Even the Lord of Speech is not capable of proclaiming the entirety of its divisions. Even the MahÄnubhÄva sages teach one portion of it in [their] Å›Ästra.27

Therefore, wise HemÄdri, here, in his own Å›Ästra, speaks the particular dharma [called] the universal dharma, distinguished by the desire or non-desire for fruit, which they traditionally hold to be obligatory (nitya) and optional (kÄmya).

In just these few verses, by drawing new connections and dissolving old boundaries, HemÄdri deliberately upends almost the entirety of the scholastic conversation about what constitutes dharma. Just like it was in Gautama, dharma is singular, but there is no mention of the Veda. It is that single and comprehensive dharma that is sub speciated into six distinctive modes, each of which has its own subcategories. Unlike the highly restricted canon proposed by someone like AparÄrka, where everything beyond the designated sources and topics is forbidden to the DharmaÅ›Ästrin or indeed any righteous person, HemÄdri leaves open a space for the seemingly subordinate inclusion of new topics, practices, and concerns, provided they can be aligned with his guiding framework. It is for this reason that he says that even the god of speech himself could not tackle the whole subject.

By far the most radical assertionâ€”entirely without precedentâ€”however, is what comes next. HemÄdri affirms that when he will speak of dharma, he is discussing the same problematic that the MahÄnubhÄva sages teach in their own Å›Ästras. This is perhaps the first time (at least outside of Tamil Nadu and some Krama teachers of Kashmir) that a wholly vernacular religious tradition, writing and teaching almost exclusively in Old Marathi, is being openly acknowledged as producing not merely legitimate knowledge of some sort, but precisely the same sort of religious discourse that one finds in BrÄhmaá¹‡ical Å›Ästra. HemÄdri acknowledges that MahÄnubhÄvika dharmaâ€”one might even call it religionâ€”is the same type of phenomena as the dharma he teaches and tackles many of the same topics. This evaluation is rendered all the more extraordinary when we recognize that within the decade, at least according to some recensions of the LÄ«á¸·Äcaritra, at the council at Devgiri, HemÄdri will try the MahÄnubhÄvika living god Cakradhar for highly particular violations of dharma, and have him [[P1594]] maimed or executed.28 Though the matter will have to be addressed in detail in another venue, at least as this trial as depicted in our surviving MahÄnubhÄvika sources, the very manner in which Cakradhar is taken to trial, namely, not because he is charged with practicing a deviant mode of conduct (ÄcÄra) that has to be suppressed, but instead for a single violation of dharma norms,29 presupposes the acceptance of the MahÄnubhÄvikas as participants within a shared paradigm of religion and values. The paradigm in question is none other than the express topic of the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i itself: the universal or common dharma.

Now, which are these six types of dharma and which one of those will we teach in this Å›Ästra? Thus, it is said [we will teach] the particular type of dharma known of as sÄdhÄraá¹‡a dharma.30

In conformity with dharma, in the Bhaviá¹£yat PurÄá¹‡a [it is stated]: There is remembered to be 1) a single vará¹‡adharma. In addition to that, [there is the dharma of] 2) ÄÅ›ramas, but 3) vará¹‡ÄÅ›rama dharma is the third. Furthermore, the occasional dharma is a secondary usage [and not one of the six species of dharma].

That dharma that operates by having recourse to a unitary class identity, that is said to be vará¹‡adharma, O king, as in the case of the upanayana.

That dharma that operates by having recourse to the stages of life is indeed ÄÅ›ramadharma: begging for alms, the walking stick, and the like.

That dharma that operates in reference to oneâ€™s vará¹‡a and ÄÅ›rama status [[P1595]] Is the dharma of vará¹‡a and ÄÅ›rama, as in the case of the mauÃ±jÄ« belt.

That dharma that operates through the qualities is called 4) guá¹‡adharma, as in the case of the protecting of the people on the part of one who has had the royal unction. That dharma that operates on the basis of a single [causal factor] is known as 5) occasional/circumstantial (nimittika), as for instance the injunction concerning expiation.31

As HemÄdri explains in his commentary on these verses, the reason we need to subdivide dharma modalities into six categories, all of which taken together amount to a unitary dharma discourse, is that in each of them we are specifying the governing causal factor. This is why we need three separate categories for vará¹‡adharma, ÄÅ›ramadharma, and then again, vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma, because in the first case, we are talking only about domains where a single caste identityâ€™s strictures are relevant and in the second, we are talking about instances where only the rules for the stage of life are relevant. It is only in the last case, our familiar notion of vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma, which is hardly being equated with DharmaÅ›Ästra let alone dharma discourse in toto, that conduct is conditioned by both class and stage of life. In other words, this is far cry from the reification of vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma we have presumed animates â€œBrÄhmaá¹‡icalâ€ discourse.

The fourth case is even more interesting, for it again conflicts with our general presuppositions about the sort of social and ideological program a BrÄhmaá¹‡ical â€œrestorationâ€ would be advocating. The so-called â€œguá¹‡adharma,â€ which again must be completely logically distinct from vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma or any of its components, apparently refers to those dharmas and their required modes of comportment that are â€œacquiredâ€ and retained as qualities by an [[P1596]] agent. And, just as upanayana must be a classified as a vará¹‡adharma, because how it is to be conducted is determined according to the vará¹‡a of the one receiving the sacred thread, with different ages and material objects being specified for each class, so too does the guá¹‡adharma receive its own illustratory example. What we learn is that the guá¹‡adharma â€œprotecting the people,â€ the role of kings and other big men in the DharmaÅ›Ästra, has as its cause the receiving of a royal unction. As this framework makes explicit, it is not conditioned by vará¹‡a, otherwise we would expect the text to posit a vará¹‡aguá¹‡adharma in the exact same way it specified a vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma. In other words, unlike in our normative presentation of a vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharma centered worldview, HemÄdri and his sources do not seem to think that being a Ká¹£atriya is logically related in any way to being a king.

The final, explicitly labelled modality, â€œoccasional or circumstantial dharmas,â€ need not detain us here, though it is worth noting that HemÄdriâ€™s definition in his commentary does indeed provide us with further indications that the printed PrÄyaÅ›cittakhaá¹‡á¸a, nowhere even alluded to in the rest of CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i, is not by his hand, for it teaches a divergent model. What is more important is that, at least in the text as supplied by HemÄdri, if you have been keeping count, the Bhaviá¹£yat PurÄá¹‡a has only listed five of its six modalities of dharma. Conspicuously missing is precisely the topic in which HemÄdri is most invested, namely, the sÄdhÄraá¹‡a or sÄmÄnya dharma. This is the actual domain where the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i seeks to establish itself as indispensable. The omission is so unexpected that it is strongly suggestive that the Bhaviá¹£yat PurÄá¹‡a itself either specifies a different sixth category of dharma entirely or said something HemÄdri did not agree with on the subject. In any case, by turning to another resource, namely, the MahÄbhÄrata, HemÄdri is able to steer the conversation in his preferred direction. [[P1597]]

But in the MahÄbhÄrata, 6) sÄdhÄraá¹‡adharma [is discussed]:

H: What is not stated and needs to be supplied is that these apply to all four vará¹‡as.33

As James Fitzgerald has rather kindly pointed out, this passage, while not an interpolation per se, occupies a somewhat marginal position within the ÅšÄntiparvan. There it emerges in the context of a teaching given to Yudhiá¹£á¹­hira taken from the song of the sage ParÄÅ›ara as it was related to King Janaka.34 The teachings on sÄdhÄraá¹‡a dharma make up only the tiniest fraction even of this discourse. Janaka requests from ParÄÅ›ara instruction in what is essentially proto-DharmaÅ›Ästra, a discourse that contains many eccentric elements. The sage responds by retelling the puruá¹£asÅ«ktaâ€™s account of the creation of the vará¹‡as. This he supplements with a Manu-esque account of the generation of what we called jÄtis from vará¹‡a mixing. He also offers an odd explanation of the origin of gotras among BrÄhmaá¹‡as, subdividing them into the four foundational groupings of Aá¹…giras, KaÅ›yapa, VaÅ›iá¹£á¹­ha, and Bhá¹›gu on the one hand, and those gotras that arose because of the deeds of different sages on the other. Janaka is then taught the specific duties of the vará¹‡as, pretty much verbatim from Ä€pastamba. It is only after this that, of his own accord, ParÄÅ›ara mentions the existence of the common or universal dharmas that apply to all vará¹‡as. The major focus of the text, however, is in exploring how and why twice-borns decline in status from undertaking the wrong sorts of activities. At the same time, the text also clarifies that, though ÅšÅ«dras do not obtain merit from [[P1598]] following Vedic injunctions (which are expressly forbidden to them), this does not mean they do not obtain merit at all. After dispensing with an apparently rival theory that suggested that ÅšÅ«dras were actually created by a whole different deity called Ka, ParÄÅ›ara proclaims that ÅšÅ«dras, like everything else in the universe, were created by Viá¹£á¹‡u. In what is essentially a pretty pro-ÅšÅ«dra stance for a work of aspirational early DharmaÅ›Ästra, ParÄÅ›ara informs Janaka that ÅšÅ«dras who maintain their purity and do pious (but not Vedic) deeds, like anyone else, reside happily in this world and ascend to the next one.

Since Janaka is apparently confused with how this stance can reconciled with the idea that ÅšÅ«dras are born in a degraded status, ParÄÅ›ara offers further clarification. There are two ways that a person can stain themselves, namely, through foul deeds or by birth. The former is a much more heinous transgression than the latter. Indeed, ParÄÅ›ara proclaims, with real admiration, that â€œhe who is defiled by birth,â€ in other words a ÅšÅ«dra, â€œbut does not do evil deeds is truly a man.â€ Prefiguring Yudhiá¹£á¹­hiraâ€™s own commitments, Janaka now asks about the virtues of ahiá¹ƒsÄ and non-cruelty and how they fit into this schema, at which point ParÄÅ›ara seems to advocate for a fireless form of asceticism animated by ethical virtues and austerities. The discourse concludes with the following words, here provided in Fitzgeraldâ€™s translation:

The message, in other words, is clear. There is such a thing as a universal ethical dharma that applies to all human beings, or at least everyone within the vará¹‡a domain. When it is adhered to (and this includes an embrace of noncruelty), living beings of all sorts go to heaven, a reward that even this early may well be conceptually distinct from liberation as such. [[P1599]] But, as the sage is equally clear in communicating, â€œyou need not ponder this.â€ In other words, this doctrine of the sÄdhÄraá¹‡adharmas is not the sort of thing exalted people like us focus on or write about.

Subsequent discourses heard this message loud and clear. While not unknown in the earlier dharma literature, sÄdhÄraá¹‡a or sÄmÄnya dharmaâ€”the two are used interchangeablyâ€”typically refers to a bare minimum baseline of shared human values upon which one can construct a particularized vision of various human dharmas inflected on the basis of caste and class. For almost all of Å›Ästra, up until the CVC, it is a marginal topic whose self-evident contents demands no further explication. In fact, in practice, the concept often serves as a rationale for holding those who fall outside of the caste system culpable for crimes they have committed, as for example in the ninth-century Ä€gamaprÄmÄá¹‡ya of the proto-ÅšrÄ«vaiá¹£á¹‡ava theologian YÄmunÄcÄrya.

The first option cannot be accepted, because the reason is unfounded. For, there is no one among men who is not eligible in certain cases for a Åšrauta practiceâ€”because, a human being as such is already eligible for nonviolence, etc. For, otherwise, Caá¹‡á¸Älas and the like who are engaging in BrÄhmaá¹‡a killing, appropriating their money, intercourse with women of caste, studying the Vedas, etc., could not be defiled, because they would not be the eligible agents with regard to these [offenses]. When a certain person ought not to do a certain thing, their doing so results in sin. Thus, the reason [in the syllogism]â€”that there exists on the part of everyone eligibility for this sort of Vedic practiceâ€”is unfounded, and the example has a defective reason.36

Even closer to our period, in the BrahmacÄrikÄá¹‡á¸a division that opens the twelfth-century Ká¹›tyakalpataru,37 using many of the same citations HemÄdri will deploy, [[P1600]] Laká¹£mÄ«dhara outlines the universal dharmas without even glossing the term or offering any commentary. Succinctly, the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡i takes this tertiary topic and expounds upon it exclusively for thousands of pages. This in of itself represents a radical innovation that breaks from the norms of the dharma knowledge system.

As should soon become apparent, however, HemÄdri is invested in theorizing the common dharma for much loftier reasons. Quite simply, envisioning an alternative mode of adhikÄra defined by ethical excellence instead of birthright, on the grounds that the cultivation of virtues and personal purity are actionable by all four castes, he effectively incorporates the concerns and commitments of non-twice-borns within a big-tent BrÄhmaá¹‡ism. By expanding the definition of the sÄmÄnya dharma so as to incorporate not just the bare minimum standard of moral behavior, but the particular lofty virtues typically associated with BrÄhmaá¹‡a-hood, HemÄdri gradually develops a rationale for claiming that even ÅšÅ«dras and women, so long as they act with absolute detachment, can potentially obtain the same soteriological ends as a pakka Vaidika BrÄhmaá¹‡a, even if they must acquire them through different means. As we might expect, the alternative modalities prescribed are none other than vrata, dÄna, tÄ«rthayÄtrÄ and worshipping the deities in mÅ«rtis and temples, the very topics which our text sets out to decisively incorporate as integral to both the practice of dharma and the discipline of DharmaÅ›Ästra. The catch, of course, is that the ability and requirement that non twice-borns can participate alongside BrÄhmaá¹‡as in a shared religious system comes at the expense of a loss of agency and sovereignty. In all matters with worldly implications, the BrÄhmaá¹‡as will always be in charge.

The MahÄbhÄrataâ€™s list of the sÄdhÄraá¹‡adharmasâ€”Å›rÄddha ritual, austerity, truth, non-anger, being content with oneâ€™s own wife, purity, being constantly without envy, ÄtmajÃ±Äna and patienceâ€”which HemÄdri will soon refine and expand, is on the surface an [[P1601]] imperfect platform for his agenda.38 Though he never acknowledges this fact, the first of its elements directly conflicts with our authorâ€™s own theorization of Å›rÄddha. In a similar manner, the explicit mention of behavior towards a wifeâ€”in place of sexual continence in the abstract, for exampleâ€”undercuts the â€œuniversalityâ€ of the practice by effectively excluding all forms of professional ascetics. Besides the authoritativeness of the MahÄbhÄrata itself, the most likely reason this particular text has been selected is that it rather atypically includes ÄtmajÃ±Änaâ€”what HemÄdri understands as knowledge of the self-acquired through VedÄntic style inquiryâ€”within the universal dharma. In any case, to alleviate such limitations, in his usual fashion, HemÄdri directs us to read the text through the lens provided by a passage from a work of Smá¹›ti, as taught by the sage Devala, in which the actual term vrata is prominently featured.

H: Tapas means the cÄndrÄyaá¹‡a fast and so forth.39

[[P1602]]

As we will learn later in this chapter when we place HemÄdri into conversation with his immediate predecessors in the world of DharmaÅ›Ästra, nearly every feature of this passage is an innovation. Such novelty starts with this new distinction drawn between tapas, comprised of the cÄndrÄyaá¹‡a fast and so forth, and another activity HemÄdri labels vrata. In the Upasmá¹›tis, just as we find in ÅšabarasvÄmin and those who follow him, like Manuâ€™s commentator MedhÄtithi, the cÄndrÄyaá¹‡a fast is not merely not representative of a separate activity called tapas. Furthermore, for all of these authorities, the category of vrata, identical to tapas, is strictly confined to the small canon of penance fasts found in these texts, which amount to a form of expiation (prÄyaÅ›citta). All other forms of vrata are condemned. Heedless of such precedents, forging his own way, HemÄdri simply takes the word vrata and makes it mean what he wants it to mean. And what he makes it mean, apart from the fast (upavÄsa) is the whole of religious practices (snÄnadÄnajapahomapÅ«jopavÄsÄdi). Until now these practices have never been included under this rubric, but a good deal of them were already integral to the lived religion and social systems of the Åšaiva Age.

Skipping over all the other terms, as for the moment they are irrelevant, HemÄdri offers one final comment on the topic of ÄtmajÃ±Äna, which offers a painful testament to what we lost with the disappearance of the CVCâ€™s Moká¹£akhaá¹‡á¸a. Now that he has established the focus of his workâ€”the explication of the common or universal dharmasâ€”HemÄdri turns to justifying his implicit claim that ÅšÅ«dras and, as we shall soon see, women can achieve liberation, despite their inability to receive the teaching directly from the Åšruti. As we are about to see, working within a MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsaka-inflected interpretive framework, HemÄdri first attempts to establish that the restrictive injunctions in the revealed texts limiting the access of women and ÅšÅ«dras to liberating knowledge pertain merely to specific, but necessary, substances and component actions involved in Vedic praxis. To accomplish this task HemÄdri has to take on the position articulated in the traditions of PÅ«rva and Uttara MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ denying ritual eligibilty (adhikÄra) to non-twice-borns. In the former discourse, this is typically labelled the Å›Å«drÄdhikaraá¹‡a, (JMS 6.7), though in actuality parts of the discussion spill over from the preceding rebuttals demonstrating that non-human entities and women should not be considered to have ritual eligibility. In the case of the [[P1605]] BrahmasÅ«tras of BÄdarÄyaá¹‡a 1.3.9, this is called the apaÅ›Å«drÄdhikaraá¹‡a. HemÄdriâ€™s argument is quite clearly offered as a direct rejoinder to the commentary and subcommentaries on the ÅšÄrÄ«rikamÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ of Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya, itself a commentary on the BrahmasÅ«tras, for it in fact harnesses a passing aside in that text and develops it into its siddhÄnta. Yet, to make sense of what is at stake, we have to briefly turn back to the older conversations in Jaimini and Åšabara.

Åšabara begins by addressing whether animals and non-human entities, be they trees, sages or godsâ€”all of whom the Veda depicts as â€œsacrificingâ€â€”can actually perform Vedic sacrifices. After first considering, and then questioning, the position that animals cannot perform sacrifices because they have no sense of the existence of the future, a position he seems to reject, he arrives at the conclusion that they cannot sacrifice, not on the basis of their cognitive capacities or lack thereof, but because they cannot carry out the entirety of the details that make up the prescribed act. In order to be able to eligible to do a ritual, in other words, you have to able to do all of it. On this basis, Åšabara shows, we can assert that not only animals and treesâ€”which for example lack handsâ€”but also the gods and the sages are not eligible to perform the rituals. In later case, this is because ritual involves the identification of the deity and the delineation of the sacrificerâ€™s gotra. Since gods, Åšabara says, cannot sacrifice to themselves, and sages, such as Bhá¹›gu, do not actually have a gotra (since we cannot say they descend from themselves), all such agents are not able to sacrifice.

Åšabara next responds to a series of objectors who take the position that women cannot sacrifice because they are a form of property, a position he rejects as unfounded. Ultimately, we learn that women, so long as they are assisting their husbands, can sacrifice and that they can benefit from the sacrifice, precisely because they are capable of fulfilling all of their assigned ritual responsibilities and meet the requirements of â€œdesiring heaven.â€ This section [[P1606]] concludes, however, with plainly stating that women can sacrifice only in so much as they perform a delimited set of tasks. We need to recognize they are in no way equal to men. After some tertiary matters concerning how many people can sacrifice, the MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ SÅ«tras and Åšabara BhÄá¹£ya at last turn to the consideration of the ritual eligibility of ÅšÅ«dras.

One of the strange things about this part of the text, usually ignored in the secondary literature, is that Jaimini himself in the sÅ«tras sees the need to respond at least six times to pÅ«rvapaká¹£ins defending the idea that ÅšÅ«dras are eligible for Vedic ritual. In the BrahmasÅ«tras of BÄdarÄyaá¹‡a as commented upon by Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya, a different pÅ«rvapaká¹£in picks up this same line of argumentation pretty much where the previous [[P1607]] authorities had left off. Much of the rest of the Åšaá¹…karaâ€™s commentary, which need not detain us, is dedicated to offering a series of rather stretched reinterpretations of seemingly positive uses of the word [[P1608]] â€œÅšÅ«draâ€ in the Upaniá¹£ads as having a metaphorical sense, as well as an invocation of the rules from the Manusmá¹›ti that assert that molten lead and lac should be poured into the ears of any ÅšÅ«dra who overhears the Veda. Now as Anand Venkatkrishnan as recently demonstrated in an unpublished paper historicizing the Å›ravaá¹‡avidhi, such a notion is not yet present in Åšaá¹…kara. He denies that the Upaniá¹£ads contain injunctions at all. It is also rejected by his disciples. Instead, the doctrine probably first appears in the eleventh-century PaÃ±capÄdikÄvivaraá¹‡a of PrakÄÅ›Ätman, which comments upon PadmapÄdaâ€™s PaÃ±capÄdikÄ.47 In Åšaá¹…karaâ€™s disciple PadmapÄda, however, the meaning of Å›rotavya and so forth, â€œone must hear,â€ is already treated as aligned with a threefold process of Å›ravaá¹‡a, manana, and nididhyÄsana. Just as importantly for our purposes, when PadmapÄda sets out to interpret this act of Å›ravaá¹‡a, he equates the act with two things, namely, learning his teacher Åšaá¹…karaâ€™s BhÄá¹£ya and, more pertinently for our purposes, â€œthe inquiry into the meaning of VedÄnta statements (vedÄntavÄkyavicÄra) that have as their purpose coming to know the nature of the Ä€tman (ÄtmÄvagataye).48

[[P1610]] As Venkatkrishnan has demonstrated, it is only in PrakÄÅ›Ätmanâ€™s sub-sub-commentary on PadmapÄdaâ€™s own subcommentary upon the first five BrahmasÅ«tras that scholastic VedÄnta begins to rigorously theorize the logical relationship between how its texts are structured and the actual practice of VedÄntic study, a task he carries out throughâ€”surprise, surpriseâ€”a renewed investment in the use of MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ reading strategies.49 And what PrakÄÅ›Ätman begins by doing is demonstrating that the very first sÅ«tra of the BrahmasÅ«tras, to quote Venkatkrishnan, â€œencodes an entire program of scriptural study that was initially articulated in the Upaniá¹£ads themselves.â€50 What he concludes is that VedÄntic vicÄra has for its textual foundation Bá¹›hadÄraá¹‡yaka Upaniá¹£ad 2.4 and what it teaches is not merely that one should â€œhearâ€ the Upaniá¹£ads, but one should study them, and that one should do it in a particular way. Moreover, what this study entails is a specific method, not superimposed from above, but allegedly derived from the source texts themselves. Ultimately, it is this method, and not the mere fact that a text is an Upaniá¹£ad, that makes the vedÄntavÄkyas serve as means to knowledge of the self. Or, as PrakÄÅ›Ätman puts it (in Venkatkrishnanâ€™s translation), right after you hear the first BrahmasÅ«tra, â€œImplicitly (arthÄt), one realizes that knowledge of Brahman is the means to liberation for a qualified student. Proximity (sannidhÄna) suggests inquiry into the statements of VedÄnta.â€51

PrakÄÅ›Ätman then takes this observation and deploys it in the service of a very different sort of argument, one that establishes that the statement about Å›ravaá¹‡a is not merely an injunction (vidhi) that tells the qualified agent he has to do something, but is actually a restrictive injunction (niyamavidhi) that informs the qualified agent that he has to do something [[P1611]] one particular way, forbidding all other alternatives.52 As you may have recognized, PrakÄÅ›Ätmanâ€™s position looks very much like that of HemÄdriâ€™s own imagined objector. HemÄdriâ€™s solution is to suggest that all that Åšruti teaches is that it is the knowledge of the self that yields liberation. What the tradition has taught is in fact merely that the inquiry into the VedÄntic statements is the most convenient method whereby one certain audience can obtain that knowledge and, with it, liberation. Since forms of vidyÄ that communicate the knowledge of the self are also found in the PurÄá¹‡as and ItihÄsa, there is no reason that â€œdeliberation (vicÄra) on the assemblage (nicaya) of statements in PurÄá¹‡as and so forthâ€ would not yield the same result as inquiry into VedÄntic statements. Either we should reject that the Å›ravaá¹‡avidhi is a restrictive injunction, or we should accept that it finds its complement in another Å›ravaá¹‡avidhi that has a different scope of applicability. This, HemÄdri suggests, is none other than the statement that â€œone should cause the four classes to hearâ€ the ItihÄsa and PurÄá¹‡as. This too can be seen as a mandatory ritual obligation for any fit person, belonging to the four vará¹‡as, who desires liberation. Once again, by â€œcausing to hear,â€ what HemÄdri really means is that the person should be guided through an inquiry process analogous to the one used by VedÄntins.

HemÄdri concludes by reading this whole process through the distinctions between vará¹‡a, ÄÅ›rama, and vará¹‡ÄÅ›ramadharmas we encountered earlier in the outline of the six modes of dharma, all of which form a single unity collectively called dharma. Just as the existence of some cases where there are rules inflected and determined by rules pertaining to a single class does not negate the existence of other sets of strictures where class and stage of life both inflect what is to be done (as when the Vedic texts give us different time spans for studentship [[P1612]] for a BrÄhmaá¹‡a, Ká¹£atriya, and VaiÅ›ya, or different ages at which the upanayana is to be given), it is the same way with the rules governing moká¹£a. There is, on the one hand, a general rule, that liberation is always accomplished by means of acquiring knowledge of the self (ÄtmajÃ±Äna). But that rule, in turn, has class-specific inflections that sometimes involve restrictions prohibiting the use of certain substances or engaging in specific actions. For non-twice-borns then, the path to liberation is indeed open, provided, of course, they restrict themselves to a program of inquiry that excludes Vedic texts.

Let us take a step back from all the MÄ«mÄá¹ƒsaka minutiae and take stock of what just happened. Probably for the first time in an extant Sanskrit treatise composed by an allegedly orthodox authority, HemÄdri has just argued that the same process of contemplative deliberation (vicÄra) on the profound statements in the Upaniá¹£ads that make possible liberation, and that VedÄnta treats as applicable to a highly delimited canon of Åšruti passages, can be transposed onto other kinds of texts. He has done this in a scholastic language that meets the requirements of learned discourse, but the end result is that he has normalized the mode of learning about â€œHinduâ€ doctrine one was most likely to encounter on the ground in twentieth-century South Asia. Astoundingly, though, much as we saw in ParÄÅ›ara GÄ«tÄ from the MahÄbhÄrata, some notion of the existence of such traditions had been in the air for over a millennium, until the CVC this topic seems never to have been written about by anyone, even with the intent of refuting the position.

At the same time, HemÄdri has also introduced us to one of the primary compositional strategies he will adopt throughout the remainder of his nibandha: argumentation through extensive exemplifications. Through piling up (nicaya) textual citations from the PurÄá¹‡as and [[P1613]] so forth, as if they were the bricks making up a Vedic altar, and then subjecting them to inquiry, HemÄdri does not merely limit himself to making evident the particular qualities of the common dharmas. In fact, he effectively reimagines the diverse social and religious ecologies of the thirteenth century as co-existing within an integrated and totalizing interpretive framework where they are subject to a single evaluatory standard.

And though HemÄdri has spoken about these matters in a learned language, as his text now makes clear, it is evident that his inspiration for this at the time eccentric position originates not from his reading of VedÄntic exegesis, but from his own study of works of PurÄá¹‡a and ItihÄsa, namely, the BhÄgavata PurÄá¹‡a and the MahÄbhÄrata.

Though we have no time to pursue this possibility here, the BhÄgavata in fact may also be providing HemÄdri with the very notion of second type for Å›ravaá¹‡a and vicÄra that has the PurÄá¹‡as as their focus, for a similar notion, albeit not articulated in the language of scholastic VedÄnta, is proposed in its opening chapters. Returning to the opening chapter of the CaturvargacintÄmaá¹‡iâ€™s Vratakhaá¹‡á¸a, setting these matters to the side, HemÄdri resumes his discussions of the nature of the universal dharma, which is above all else a universal code of ethical comportment. As by now should be plain, HemÄdriâ€™s understanding of the universal and acts of universalization carries a substantively different connotation than we might first anticipate. Once HemÄdri has explained just what he means by sÄmÄnya or sÄdhÄraá¹‡a dharma, his attention switches to two different tasks.
